# Announcing textual-plotext

A common query on the [Textual Discord](https://discord.gg/Enf6Z3qhVr) is terminal plotting. While **Plotext** lacks direct Textual support, it integrates well with **Rich**, inspiring `textual-plotext`—a library enabling Plotext plots in Textual apps.

### Overview

`textual-plotext` provides the `PlotextPlot` widget for embedding Plotext visualizations in Textual applications. It mirrors conventional Plotext usage:

```python
from textual.app import App, ComposeResult
from textual_plotext import PlotextPlot

class ScatterApp(App[None]):
    def compose(self) -> ComposeResult:
        yield PlotextPlot()

    def on_mount(self) -> None:
        plt = self.query_one(PlotextPlot).plt
        y = plt.sin()
        plt.scatter(y)
        plt.title("Scatter Plot")
```

No `build()` or `show()` calls needed—`textual-plotext` handles rendering automatically.

### Features

- Full compatibility with Plotext API (except animated GIFs and videos[^1]).
- Direct access to `plt` via `PlotextPlot.plt`.
- Supports all standard Plotext plot types.
- Seamlessly integrates with Textual’s worker system for async data loading.

### Examples

Included demo showcases plots from the Plotext README:

![Demo 1](/blog/images/textual-plotext/demo1.png)  
![Demo 2](/blog/images/textual-plotext/demo2.png)  
![Demo 3](/blog/images/textual-plotext/demo3.png)  
![Demo 4](/blog/images/textual-plotext/demo4.png)

Long-form example: [Textual Towers weather app](https://github.com/Textualize/textual-plotext/blob/main/examples/textual_towers_weather.py), using workers to fetch and plot historical data.

![Weather history app](/blog/images/textual-plotext/weather.png)

### Use Cases

- Existing Plotext users: Easily convert scripts into full terminal apps.
- Textual developers: Add rich, interactive plots with minimal friction.

For questions or discussion, join the [Textual Discord](https://discord.gg/Enf6Z3qhVr) or [GitHub Discussions](https://github.com/Textualize/textual/discussions).

[^1]: Animated GIFs and video plots not supported.

## Embedded Code Examples

### `inline_python`

```
import plotext as plt
y = plt.sin() # sinusoidal test signal
plt.scatter(y)
plt.title("Scatter Plot") # to apply a title
plt.show() # to finally plot

```

### `inline_python`

```
from textual.app import App, ComposeResult

from textual_plotext import PlotextPlot

class ScatterApp(App[None]):

    def compose(self) -> ComposeResult:
        yield PlotextPlot()

    def on_mount(self) -> None:
        plt = self.query_one(PlotextPlot).plt
        y = plt.sin() # sinusoidal test signal
        plt.scatter(y)
        plt.title("Scatter Plot") # to apply a title

if __name__ == "__main__":
    ScatterApp().run()

```

#### textual-serve-files.md

**Title:** title: "Towards Textual Web Applications"

**Path:** `blog/posts/textual-serve-files.md`

**Content:**

```text
---
draft: false  
date: 2024-09-08  
categories:
  - DevLog  
authors:
  - darrenburns  
title: "Towards Textual Web Applications"  

<!-- more -->

## What is `textual-serve`?

[`textual-serve`](https://github.com/Textualize/textual-serve) serves Textual apps via browser using WebSocket, running the app on a controlled machine. End-users access it through a URL without direct machine access.

Example: Run [`harlequin`](https://github.com/tconbeer/harlequin) or [`posting`](https://github.com/darrenburns/posting) on a server with `textual-serve`, share the URL—users can query databases or send HTTP requests from their browser, all executed on the host machine.

<figure markdown>
![posting running in a browser](../images/textual-serve-files/posting-textual-serve.png)
  <figcaption>Accessing Posting via web browser.</figcaption>
</figure>

## Equal Experience Across Platforms

Textual apps run server-side; users interact via terminal or browser. To ensure consistent UX, APIs abstract platform differences.

Developers need not detect runtime environment—`App.open_url`, `deliver_text`, and `deliver_binary` provide cross-platform file & link handling.

## Opening Web Links

[`App.open_url`](https://textual.textualize.io/api/app/#textual.app.App.open_url) enables opening URLs:
- **Terminal**: Uses Python’s [`webbrowser`](https://docs.python.org/3/library/webbrowser.html).
- **Browser**: `textual-serve` relays request via WebSocket to open link in user's browser.

No conditional logic needed—behavior adapts automatically.

## Saving Files to Disk

[`App.deliver_text`](https://textual.textualize.io/api/app/#textual.app.App.deliver_text) and [`App.deliver_binary`](https://textual.textualize.io/api/app/#textual.app.App.deliver_binary) deliver files:
- **Terminal**: Writes file to disk, notifies app.
- **Browser**: Triggers download via ephemeral URL; supports custom filename, MIME type, and open-in-tab behavior.

File delivery is platform-agnostic by design.

## How It Works

### Input Handling
Textual uses "drivers":
- **Local terminal drivers** (Linux/Windows): Read `stdin`, parse ANSI escape sequences from terminal emulator → generate Textual events.
- **Web driver**: Runs in browser using [`xterm.js`](https://xtermjs.org/) as terminal emulator. User input → ANSI codes → WebSocket → `textual-serve` → app’s `stdin`.

### Output Handling
App writes to `stdout`, processed by:
- **Local**: Terminal emulator renders output.
- **Web**: `stdout` sent via WebSocket; `xterm.js` renders in browser.

Data flow: ANSI sequences (and arbitrary payloads) over WebSocket.

### File Delivery Protocol
When `deliver_text` or `deliver_binary` is called:
1. App signals file ready.
2. `textual-serve` generates ephemeral, one-time download URL.
3. URL sent via WebSocket to client.
4. Browser streams file using Bencode-encoded chunks (BitTorrent-style encoding).

Streaming ensures secure, efficient delivery without persistent storage.

![textual-serve-overview](../images/textual-serve-files/textual-serve-overview.png)

## Result

These APIs close the feature gap between terminal and web: apps can be accessed via either platform with full functionality preserved—no extra effort required from developers.

## Found t  # Limit input size for API calls
```

# What is Textual Web?

The team behind [Rich](https://github.com/Textualize/rich), [Textual](https://github.com/Textualize/textual), [Rich-CLI](https://github.com/Textualize/rich-cli), [Trogon](https://github.com/Textualize/trogon), and [Frogmouth](https://github.com/Textualize/frogmouth) introduces **textual-web**.

Textual Web converts a Textual-powered TUI into a web application. Use the `textual-web` command to publish any Textual app via URL—no local socket server required, eliminating firewall and port configuration needs.

Runs anywhere with an outgoing internet connection. Built by one Python developer with no traditional web stack experience; only requires proficiency in Python and documentation review ([Textual docs](https://textual.textualize.io/)).

Future updates will expose Web platform APIs (e.g., notifications, file system access) via unified Python interfaces—same code works across terminal, browser, and desktop. Supports PWA development for single-codebase TUI, web, and desktop apps.

Currently in public beta. Join the [Discord server](https://discord.gg/Enf6Z3qhVr) to test or ask questions.

#### to-tui-or-not-to-tui.md

# To TUI or not to TUI

Terminals are a persistent technology. Text User Interfaces (TUIs)—interactive terminal-based applications—are central to modern developer tooling.

At [Textualize](https://textual.textualize.io/), we explore TUI potential in the broader tech ecosystem. In April, we paused work on [Textual](https://github.com/Textualize/textual) to build two TUI apps using it—each taking ~3 dev-weeks (including Textual improvements and community support).

### Projects

**Frogmouth** ([GitHub](https://github.com/Textualize/frogmouth))  
A Markdown browser. Outperforms web equivalents in many aspects; missing only image rendering (coming soon).  

![Frogmouth](--8<-- "docs/blog/images/frogmouth.svg")  
*Note: SVGs exported from Textual, not screenshots.*

Released Sunday (US time), gained 1K stars within a day, received strong community feedback.

**Trogon** ([GitHub](https://github.com/Textualize/trogon))  
A library that auto-generates TUIs for CLI applications.  

![Trogon](--8<-- "docs/blog/images/trogon.svg")  
Same release timing; reached 1K stars faster than Frogmouth.

Both projects are early-stage but show strong momentum. Star history: ![Star history](../images/star-history-trogon-frogmouth.png)

### Conclusion

TUIs no longer require high motivation to build—modern frameworks like Textual enable maintainable, standalone or integrative TUIs. As a "forever technology," TUIs remain a reliable foundation.

### Discord  
Discuss with Textualize devs: [Discord server](https://discord.gg/Enf6Z3qhVr)

#### toolong-retrospective.md

**Title:** File magic with the Python standard library

**Path:** `blog/posts/toolong-retrospective.md`

# File magic with the Python standard library

Toolong, a log viewer app, addresses large file handling via efficient I/O techniques. These are broadly applicable to any Python project.

## Opening large files

Standard `open().read()` loads entire files into memory—prohibitive for gigabyte-scale logs. Toolong avoids this by reading only data needed for visible content, enabling near-instant load times and real-time scrolling with syntax highlighting.

### Scanning lines

To enable line navigation and scrollbar rendering, Toolong must locate all `\n` offsets in the file. This is optimized using `mmap.mmap`.

```python
def scan_line_breaks(
    self, batch_time: float = 0.25
) -> Iterable[tuple[int, list[int]]]:
    """Scan file for line breaks.

    Args:
        batch_time: Time interval to group batches.

    Yields:
        Tuples of (scan position, list of line offsets).
    """
    fileno = self.fileno
    size = self.size
    if not size:
        return
    log_mmap = mmap.mmap(fileno, size, prot=mmap.PROT_READ)
    rfind = log_mmap.rfind
    position = size
    batch: list[int] = []
    append = batch.append
    get_length = batch.__len__
    monotonic = time.monotonic
    break_time = monotonic()

    while (position := rfind(b"\n", 0, position)) != -1:
        append(position)
        if get_length() % 1000 == 0 and monotonic() - break_time > batch_time:
            break_time = monotonic()
            yield (position, batch)
            batch = []
            append = batch.append
    yield (0, batch)
    log_mmap.close()
```

Runs in a worker thread. Batching prevents UI lag from millions of events. `rfind` operates at C speed; OS handles disk reads.

## Watching files for changes

Real-time tailing uses `selectors`, which efficiently detects file readiness via OS-level notifications (e.g., `KqueueSelector` on macOS/Linux). Unlike polling, it avoids CPU waste and enables near-instant updates.

See [watcher.py](https://github.com/Textualize/toolong/blob/main/src/toolong/watcher.py) for implementation. On systems lacking `KqueueSelector`, fallback to polling.

## Textual learnings

Toolong served as a "dogfood" project for Textual. Improvements include:
- Better error detection for missing `super()` calls in widget constructors.
- Enhanced diagnostics for common programming pitfalls.
- Quality-of-life refinements for intuitive feature implementation.

Expect further updates in the coming weeks.

## Found this interesting?

Discuss on [Discord](https://discord.gg/Enf6Z3qhVr).

        """Scan the file for line breaks.

        Args:

        Returns:
            An iterable of tuples, containing the scan position and a list of offsets of new lines.

                batch = []
                append = batch.append

**Title:** &lt;color&gt;

```markdown
# `<color>`

The `<color>` CSS type represents a color.

!!! warning  
    Not to be confused with the [`color`](../styles/color.md) rule for text coloring.

## Syntax

A `<color>` may be specified in one of:
- A recognized [named color](#named-colors) (e.g., `red`);
- 3 or 6 hex digits (`#RRGGBB` or `#RGB`, case-insensitive);
- 4 or 8 hex digits for RGBA (`#RRGGBBAA` or `#RGBA`);
- `rgb(red, green, blue)` with values 0–255;
- `rgba(red, green, blue, alpha)` where `alpha ∈ [0,1]`;
- `hsl(hue, saturation%, lightness%)` with `hue ∈ [0,360]`, `% ∈ [0,100]`;
- `hsla(hue, saturation%, lightness%, alpha)` with `alpha ∈ [0,1]`.

[Textual’s default themes](../guide/design.md) provide CSS variables (e.g., `$accent`) for direct use.

### Named Colors

Listed in `COLOR_NAME_TO_RGB` from `textual._color_constants`. Each entry maps a name to an RGB triplet.  
Visual sample via Rich table:  
```{.rich columns="80" title="colors"}
from textual._color_constants import COLOR_NAME_TO_RGB
from textual.color import Color
from rich.table import Table
from rich.text import Text
table = Table("Name", "hex", "RGB", "Color", expand=True, highlight=True)
for name, triplet in sorted(COLOR_NAME_TO_RGB.items()):
    if len(triplet) != 3:
        continue
    color = Color(*triplet)
    r, g, b = triplet
    table.add_row(
        f'"{name}"',
        Text(f"{color.hex}", "bold green"),
        f"rgb({r}, {g}, {b})",
        Text("                    ", style=f"on rgb({r},{g},{b})")
    )
output = table
```

### Hex RGB

- `#RRGGBB`: 6-digit, channel values in hex (0–F).
- `#RGB`: 3-digit shorthand; each digit duplicated → e.g., `#A2F` ≡ `#AA22FF`.

### Hex RGBA

- `#RRGGBBAA`: 8-digit, alpha appended.
- `#RGBA`: 4-digit shorthand; each channel and alpha duplicated.

### `rgb()` / `rgba()`

- `rgb(r, g, b)` → r,g,b ∈ [0,255].
- `rgba(r, g, b, a)` → a ∈ [0,1].

Example: `rgb(0, 255, 32)` ≡ `#00FF20`.

### `hsl()` / `hsla()`

- `hsl(h, s%, l%)`:
  - h ∈ [0,360],
  - s% ∈ [0,100],
  - l% ∈ [0,100].
- `hsla(...)` adds alpha ∈ [0,1].

Example: `#00FF20` → `hsl(128, 100%, 50%)`.

## Examples

### CSS
```css
Header {
    background: red;
}
.accent {
    color: $accent;
}
#footer {
    tint: hsl(300, 20%, 70%);
}
```

### Python
Accepts `<color>` via string or `Color` instance:
```py
widget.styles.background = "red"
widget.styles.color = "$accent"
widget.styles.tint = "hsl(300, 20%, 70%)"

from textual.color import Color
color = Color(16, 200, 45)
color = Color.parse("#A8F")  # Parses CSS syntax
```
```

## Embedded Code Examples

### `inline_css`

```
Header {
    background: red;           /* Color name */
}

.accent {
    color: $accent;            /* Textual variable */
}

#footer {
    tint: hsl(300, 20%, 70%);  /* HSL description */
}

```

### `inline_py`

```
# Mimicking the CSS syntax
widget.styles.background = "red"           # Color name
widget.styles.color = "$accent"            # Textual variable
widget.styles.tint = "hsl(300, 20%, 70%)"  # HSL description

from textual.color import Color
# Using a Color object directly...
color = Color(16, 200, 45)
# ... which can also parse the CSS syntax
color = Color.parse("#A8F")

```

# `<percentage>`

The `<percentage>` CSS type represents a relative value expressed as a percentage of the parent's dimension or property.

!!! warning  
    Not to be confused with [`<scalar>`](./scalar.md).

## Syntax

A `<percentage>` is a `<number>` followed by `%` (no whitespace). Some properties constrain values to the range `0%` to `100%`.

    color: red 70%;
    offset: -30% 12.5%;
}

# `<pointer>`

The `<pointer>` CSS type defines cursor shapes displayed when the mouse hovers over a widget.

## Syntax

Valid values:
- `default` – Default pointer shape  
- `pointer` – Pointing hand (e.g., for links)  
- `text` – I-beam text selection cursor  
- `crosshair` – Crosshair cursor  
- `help` – Help indicator (typically a question mark)  
- `wait` – Busy/wait cursor  
- `progress` – Background activity indicator  
- `move` – Four-directional move cursor  
- `grab` – Open hand (grabbable state)  
- `grabbing` – Closed hand (currently grabbing)  
- `cell` – Cell selection cursor  
- `vertical-text` – Vertical text selection cursor  
- `alias` – Alias/shortcut indicator  
- `copy` – Copy operation cursor  
- `no-drop` – No drop allowed  
- `not-allowed` – Prohibited action  
- `n-resize`, `s-resize`, `e-resize`, `w-resize` – Cardinal resize cursors  
- `ne-resize`, `nw-resize`, `se-resize`, `sw-resize` – Diagonal resize cursors  
- `ew-resize`, `ns-resize` – Horizontal/vertical resize  
- `nesw-resize`, `nwse-resize` – Diagonal (NE-SW) and (NW-SE) resize  
- `zoom-in` – Magnifying glass with +  
- `zoom-out` – Magnifying glass with -  

!!! note
    `pointer` requires Kitty pointer shapes protocol support. Not all terminals implement this.

## Examples

### CSS
```css
#my-button {
    pointer: pointer;
}
```

### Python
```py
widget.styles.pointer = "pointer"
```

#### scalar.md

**Title:** &lt;scalar&gt;

**Path:** `css_types/scalar.md`

**Content:**

```text
# `<scalar>`

The `<scalar>` CSS type represents a length, either as a [`<number>`](./number.md) with unit or the special value `auto`. Used in properties like [`width`](../styles/width.md) and [`height`](../styles/height.md).

!!! warning  
    Not to be confused with [`<number>`](./number.md) or [`<percentage>`](./percentage.md).

## Syntax

A `<scalar>` may be:
- Integer or float (truncated to int): `10`
- Fractional proportion: `1fr`
- Percent relative to container: `75%`
- Width-relative percent: `25w` (→ 25% of container width)
- Height-relative percent: `75h` (→ 75% of container height)
- Viewport-width relative: `25vw` (→ 25% of viewport width)
- Viewport-height relative: `75vh` (→ 75% of viewport height)
- Special value: `auto` (optimal fit without scrolling)

| Unit | Meaning | Example | Description |
|------|--------|---------|-------------|
| `""` | Cell (absolute) | `10` | Number of columns (horizontal), lines (vertical). Floats truncated to int. |
| `"fr"` | Fractional proportion | `1fr` | Proportional sizing; e.g., `3fr` is 3× wider than `1fr`. |
| `"%"` | Percent (container-relative) | `75%` | Relative to container’s width/height. |
| `"w"` | Width-based percent | `25w` | 25% of container width; used for both width and height. |
| `"h"` | Height-based percent | `75h` | 75% of container height; used for both width and height. |
| `"vw"` | Viewport-width relative | `25vw` | Relative to terminal width (excluding docked widgets). |
| `"vh"` | Viewport-height relative | `75vh` | Relative to terminal height (excluding docked widgets). |
| - | Auto-fit | `auto` | Computes optimal size to fit content without scrolling. |

### Cell
- Absolute unit: number of columns (`width`) or lines (`height`)
- Floats are truncated to integers

### Fraction
- Proportional sizing; e.g., `1fr` vs `3fr` → 1:3 ratio

### Percent
- Relative to container dimensions:
  - `width: 50%` → 50% of container width
  - `height: 50%` → 50% of container height

### Width (`w`)
- `%` relative to container **width**
  - `width: 25w` → 25% of container width
  - `height: 25w` → 25% of container width (applies to height)

### Height (`h`)
- `%` relative to container **height**
  - `height: 75h` → 75% of container height
  - `width: 75h` → 75% of container height (applies to width)

### Viewport Width (`vw`)
- Relative to viewport width (terminal minus docked widgets)
  - `width: 25vw` → 25% of viewport width

### Viewport Height (`vh`)
- Relative to viewport height (terminal minus docked widgets)
  - `height: 75vh` → 75% of viewport height

### Auto
- Computes optimal size to fit content without scrolling:
  - e.g., label with `width: auto` matches text width if container allows.

## Examples

### CSS
```css
Horizontal {
    width: 60;     /* 60 cells */
    height: 1fr;   /* proportional (1 fraction) */
}
```

### Python
```py
widget.styles.width = 16       # Cell unit via int/float
widget.styles.height = "1fr"   # Fractional proportion as string
```

# Limit input size for API calls

## Embedded Code Examples

### `inline_css`

```
Horizontal {
    width: 60;     /* 60 cells */
    height: 1fr;   /* proportional size of 1 */
}

```

### `inline_py`

```
widget.styles.width = 16       # Cell unit can be specified with an int/float
widget.styles.height = "1fr"   # proportional size of 1

```

```

```text
# `<text-align>`

The `<text-align>` CSS type defines valid text alignment values.

!!! warning  
    Not to be confused with the [`text-align`](../styles/text_align.md) CSS rule used for widget-level text alignment.

## Syntax

A `<text-align>` accepts one of:  
`center`, `end` (alias: `right`), `justify`, `left`, `right`, `start` (alias: `left`).  

!!! tip  
    `start` and `end` behavior may change with future RTL language support in Textual.

## Examples

### CSS
```css
Label {
    text-align: justify;
}
```

### Python
```py
widget.styles.text_align = "justify"
```

## Embedded Code Examples

### `inline_css`

```
Label {
    text-align: justify;
}

```

### `inline_py`

```
widget.styles.text_align = "justify"

```

```

#### text_style.md

**Title:** &lt;text-style&gt;

**Path:** `css_types/text_style.md`

**Content:**

```text
# `<text-style>`

The `<text-style>` CSS type defines text styling options.

!!! warning

    Do not confuse with the [`text-style`](../styles/text_style.md) CSS rule used to style text in widgets.

## Syntax

A `<text-style>` is either:
- `none` (plain text, no styling), or
- A space-separated combination of:  
  `bold`, `italic`, `reverse`, `strike`, `underline`.

## Examples

### CSS

```css
#label1 {
    rule: strike;
}

#label2 {
    rule: strike bold italic reverse;
}
```

### Python

```py
widget.styles.text_style = "strike"
widget.styles.text_style = "strike bold italic reverse"
```

# Limit input size for API calls

## Embedded Code Examples

### `inline_css`

```
#label1 {
    /* You can specify any value by itself. */
    rule: strike;
}

#label2 {
    /* You can also combine multiple values. */
    rule: strike bold italic reverse;
}

```

### `inline_py`

```
# You can specify any value by itself
widget.styles.text_style = "strike"

# You can also combine multiple values
widget.styles.text_style = "strike bold italic reverse

```

```

# `<vertical>`

The `<vertical>` CSS type defines a position along the vertical axis.

## Syntax

The [`<vertical>`](./vertical.md) type accepts:  
- `bottom` — aligns at the bottom of the vertical axis.  
- `middle` — centers vertically.  
- `top` (default) — aligns at the top of the vertical axis.

## Examples

### CSS
```css
.container {
    align-vertical: top;
}
```

### Python
```py
widget.styles.align_vertical = "top"
```

}

```text
::: textual.events.Click  
    options:
      heading_level: 1

## Double & triple clicks  

The `chain` attribute of the `Click` event indicates the number of consecutive clicks within a time threshold. Values:  
- `1`: single click  
- `2`: double click  
- higher values: multiple sequential clicks  

Default threshold: **500ms**; adjust via `CLICK_CHAIN_TIME_THRESHOLD` in your `App` subclass.  

Inherits from [MouseEvent][textual.events.MouseEvent].  

## See also  

[Enter](enter.md), [Leave](leave.md), [MouseDown](mouse_down.md), [MouseMove](mouse_move.md), [MouseScrollDown](mouse_scroll_down.md), [MouseScrollLeft](mouse_scroll_left.md), [MouseScrollRight](mouse_scroll_right.md), [MouseScrollUp](mouse_scroll_up.md), [MouseUp](mouse_up.md)
```

### guide

#### CSS.md

**Title:** Textual CSS

**Path:** `guide/CSS.md`

**Content:**

```text
# Textual CSS

Textual uses CSS to style widgets. Familiarity with web development helps, but is not required.

### Stylesheets

CSS (_Cascading Style Sheets_) defines rules for styling elements. In Textual, styles apply to widgets via a tree-like DOM structure.

#### Rule Set Example
```css
Header {
  dock: top;
  height: 3;
  content-align: center middle;
  background: blue;
  color: white;
}
```

- **Selector**: `Header` targets the `Header` widget class.
- **Rules**:
  - `dock: top`: Anchors to screen’s top edge; valid values: `top`, `right`, `bottom`, `left`.
  - `height: 3`: Sets height in rows.
  - `content-align: center middle`: Centers content horizontally and vertically.
  - `background: blue`, `color: white`: Applies background and text color.

Rules are separated by semicolons; multiple rules per line allowed if properly delimited.

---

### DOM (Document Object Model)

Textual’s DOM is a hierarchical tree of widgets. Parent-child relationships form branches:
- `Screen` is child of `ExampleApp`.
- `Header`, `Footer` are children of `Screen`.

#### Example: Basic App (`dom1.py`)
```python
--8<-- "docs/examples/guide/dom1.py"
```
Output:  
```{.textual path="docs/examples/guide/dom1.py"}
```

#### Enhanced App with Header/Footer (`dom2.py`)
```python hl_lines="7 8"
--8<-- "docs/examples/guide/dom2.py"
```
Output:  
```{.textual path="docs/examples/guide/dom2.py"}
```

> **Note**: `Header` and `Footer` contain internal children; their structure is abstracted unless custom styling is needed.

#### Complex Dialog (`dom3.py`)
Uses:
- [`Container`](textual.containers.Container)
- [`Horizontal`](textual.containers.Horizontal) for layout
- [`Static`](textual.widgets.Static) for content
- [`Button`](textual.widgets.Button) for interaction

```python hl_lines="12 13 14 15 16 17 18 19 20" title="dom3.py"
--8<-- "docs/examples/guide/dom3.py"
```

DOM structure:
<div class="excalidraw">
--8<-- "docs/images/dom3.excalidraw.svg"
</div>

Output:  
```{.textual path="docs/examples/guide/dom3.py"}
```

> **Note**: Visual appearance requires CSS styling.

---

### CSS Files

Set `CSS_PATH` class variable to a relative path:
- `.tcss` extension distinguishes Textual CSS from browser CSS.
- Supports multiple files via list assignment.

#### Example (`dom4.py`)
```python hl_lines="9" title="dom4.py"
--8<-- "docs/examples/guide/dom4.py"
```

Widget constructors support `id` and `classes` kwargs for targeting in CSS.

#### CSS File (`dom4.tcss`)
```css title="dom4.tcss"
--8<-- "docs/examples/guide/dom4.tcss"
```
- Supports comments: `/* ... */`.
- Combines rules from multiple files when `CSS_PATH` is a list.

With applied stylesheet, output becomes:
```{.textual path="docs/examples/guide/dom4.py"}
```

---

### Why Use CSS?

1. **Separation of Concerns**: Decouples UI appearance from logic.
2. **Customization Flexibility**: Style any widget (built-in or third-party) identically.
3. **Live Editing**: Run with `textual run my_app.py --dev` to auto-reload CSS changes.

---

### Selectors

Target widgets via:
- **Type selector** (`Header`, `Button`) — matches class name and base classes.
  ```css
  Alert {
    border: solid red;
  }
  ```
  Where `Alert` is a subclass of `Static`.

Selectors support specificity, allowing granular control over individual or group styling.

## Embedded Code Examples

### `inline_css`

```

  dock: top;
  height: 3;
  content-align: center middle;
  background: blue;
  color: white;
}

#### actions.md

**Title:** Actions

**Path:** `guide/actions.md`

**Content:**

```text
# Actions

Actions are allow-listed functions with string syntax, embeddable in links and bindable to keys. They are defined as methods prefixed with `action_` on `App`, `Screen`, or `Widget` classes.

## Action Methods

Action methods may be coroutines (`async`). Example:
```python title="actions01.py" hl_lines="6-7 11"
--8<-- "docs/examples/guide/actions/actions01.py"
```
The method `action_set_background` sets screen background. Key handler calls it on ++r++ press.

Action strings like `"set_background('red')"` invoke `self.action_set_background('red')`. Use [run_action()][textual.widgets.Widget.run_action] to parse and dispatch:
```python title="actions02.py" hl_lines="9-11"
--8<-- "docs/examples/guide/actions/actions02.py"
```
`run_action()` is a coroutine; `on_key` must be `async`.

## Syntax

Action strings mimic Python function syntax but **do not** use `eval`. Valid formats:
- `"bell"` → calls `action_bell()`.
- `"set_background('red')"` → calls `action_set_background('red')`.
- Namespaced: `app.set_background(...)`, `screen.set_background(...)`, `focused.set_background(...)`.

### Parameters

Parameters must be valid Python literals (e.g., strings, numbers, lists, dicts). Variables or references are invalid:
- ✅ `"set_background('blue')"`
- ❌ `"set_background(new_color)"`

## Links

Embed actions in [markup](./content.md#actions) via `@click` tag:
=== "actions03.py"
    ```python title="actions03.py" hl_lines="4-9 13-14"
    --8<-- "docs/examples/guide/actions/actions03.py"
    ```
Clicking links run `"set_background"` with specified color.

## Bindings

Key bindings trigger actions:
=== "actions04.py"
    ```python title="actions04.py" hl_lines="13-17"
    --8<-- "docs/examples/guide/actions/actions04.py"
    ```
++r++, ++g++, ++b++ keys invoke `"set_background"` on screen. See [input](./input.md#bindings) for more.

## Namespaces

Action resolution:
- `app` → App actions.
- `screen` → Screen actions.
- `focused` → Currently focused widget’s actions.

In custom widgets, links like `app.set_background('red')` invoke App-level action instead of local one. Example:
=== "actions05.py"
    ```python title="actions05.py" hl_lines="13-14"
    --8<-- "docs/examples/guide/actions/actions05.py"
    ```
CSS: `actions05.tcss`
Clicking links affect only the widget; key bindings on App affect screen.

## Dynamic Actions

Introduced in version 0.61.0.

Use [`check_action`][textual.dom.DOMNode.check_action] to conditionally enable/disable actions and bindings based on state:
- Return `True`: show & run.
- Return `False`: hide & block.
- Return `None`: disable (dimmed) & block.

Example:
=== "actions06.py"
    ```python title="actions06.py" hl_lines="27 32 35-43"
    --8<-- "docs/examples/guide/actions/actions06.py"
    ```
1. `refresh_bindings()` updates footer.
2. Same as above.
3. Prevents action and hides key when invalid.

CSS: `actions06.tcss`

Output:
```{.textual path="docs/examples/guide/actions/actions06.py"}
```

Use `bindings=True` on [reactive](./reactivity.md) to auto-refresh bindings on change:
=== "actions07.py"
    ```python title="actions07.py" hl_lines="17 36 38"
    --8<-- "docs/examples/guide/actions/actions07.py"
    ```
1. `bindings=True` triggers footer refresh.
2. `None` → disables key (dimmed).
3. Same as above.

CSS: `actions06.tcss`

#### animation.md

**Title:** Animation

**Path:** `guide/animation.md`

**Content:**

```text
# Animation

Textual's animation system enables visual effects like movement, blending, and fading by interpolating attribute values over time. Animations can be applied to `styles` attributes such as `offset` (for positioning) and `opacity` (for transparency).

Both apps and widgets provide an [`animate`](textual.app.App.animate) method; `styles` objects also support the same `animate` method.

### Example: Fading a Widget
The following example animates a `Static` widget’s opacity from 1.0 to 0.0 over 2 seconds, making it fade out:

```python hl_lines="14"
--8<-- "docs/examples/guide/animator/animation01.py"
```

The animator updates the `opacity` attribute in small increments every frame until completion.

| Time | Appearance |
|------|------------|
| 0s   | Visible    |
| 1s   | Partially faded |
| 1.5s | Nearly invisible |
| 2s   | Fully invisible |

### Duration & Speed
- `duration`: Animation duration in seconds.
- `speed`: Rate of change per second (units/sec).  
  Example: Animate from 0 to 10 at speed 2 → completes in 5 seconds.

### Easing Functions
Control interpolation curve via the `easing` parameter. Default is `"in_out_cubic"` for smooth acceleration and deceleration. Supported easing types align with [easings.net](https://easings.net/).

Preview available via:
```bash
textual easing
```
> Requires `pip install textual-dev`.

### Completion Callbacks
Use the `on_complete` parameter to pass a callable executed upon animation end.

### Delayed Animations
Delay start using `delay`, accepting a `float` (seconds).  
Example: `self.box.styles.animate("opacity", value=0.0, duration=2.0, delay=5.0)` starts after 5 seconds and ends at t=7s.

## Embedded Code Examples

### `inline_bash`

```
textual easing

```

```

**Title:** App Basics

**Path:** `guide/app.md`

**Content:**

```text
# App Basics

## The App Class

Create a Textual app by subclassing [`App`][textual.app.App]. The minimal example:

```python
--8<-- "docs/examples/app/simple01.py"
```

### Run Method

Run the app via `app.run()`:

```python hl_lines="8-10" title="simple02.py"
--8<-- "docs/examples/app/simple02.py"
```

> **Note**: `__name__ == "__main__"` enables safe imports and supports [`devtools run`](devtools.md#run). See [Python docs](https://docs.python.org/3/library/__main__.html#idiomatic-usage).

Running with `python simple02.py` enters *application mode*: the terminal disables echo, Textual handles input (keyboard/mouse), and updates screen content. Press ++ctrl+q++ to exit and restore prior terminal state.

#### Inline Mode (v0.55.0+)

Run in inline mode (`inline=True`) to display app beneath prompt without entering application mode. Useful for workflow-integrated tools.  
> **Note**: Not supported on Windows.

#### ANSI Colors (v0.80.0+)

Terminals support 16 themeable ANSI colors. By default, Textual overrides them; disable via `ansi_color=True` in [`App.__init__()`][textual.app.App]. Recommended for full-screen apps; consider preserving ANSI in inline mode.

## Events

Textual’s event system responds to key presses, mouse actions, and state changes. Handlers are methods prefixed with `on_`.

### Example: Mount & Key Events

```python title="event01.py"
--8<-- "docs/examples/app/event01.py"
```

- `on_mount`: Sets background color (`self.screen.styles.background = "darkblue"`).
- `on_key`: Receives [`Key`][textual.events.Key] instance. Uses `key` attribute to detect digits 0–9 and change background.

```{.textual path="docs/examples/app/event01.py" hl_lines="23-25"}
```

### Async Events

Textual uses Python’s `asyncio`. Handlers marked `async` are awaited; regular functions suffice unless integrating async libraries (e.g., [`httpx`](https://www.python-httpx.org/)).  
> **Tip**: See FastAPI's [concurrent burgers](https://fastapi.tiangolo.com/async/) for async intro.

## Widgets

Widgets generate UI content and respond to events. Can be simple (text, button) or complex (editor, file browser).

### Composing

Override [`compose()`][textual.app.App.compose] to return an iterable of `Widget` instances. Use generator syntax (`yield`) for convenience:

```python title="widgets01.py"
--8<-- "docs/examples/app/widgets01.py"
```

Mounts a builtin `Welcome` widget (Markdown + button).  
> **Note**: `on_button_pressed` calls [`App.exit()`][textual.app.App.exit] to quit.

### Mounting

Add widgets dynamically via [`mount()`][textual.widget.Widget.mount]:

```python title="widgets02.py"
--8<-- "docs/examples/app/widgets02.py"
```

Initial screen is blank. Press any key to mount `Welcome`. Multiple presses add multiple instances.

```{.textual path="docs/examples/app/widgets02.py" press="a,a,a,down,down,down,down,down,down"}
```

#### Awaiting Mount

`mount()` mounts all composed widgets by the *next* message handler—**not immediately**. This can cause issues when modifying mounted content in same handler.

Example:
```python hl_lines="2 8"
from textual.app import App
from textual.widgets import Button, Welcome

class WelcomeApp(App):
    def on_key(self) -> None:
        self.mount(Welcome())
        self.query_one(Button).label = "YES!" # (1)!
```

> **Note**: `query_one` raises [`NoMatches`][textual.css.query.NoMatches] if widget not yet mounted. See [queries](./queries.md) for details.

*All API signatures, class hierarchies, parameter types, return types, and technical terminology preserved.*

    self.exit(return_code=4, message="Critical error occurred")

#### command_palette.md

**Title:** Command Palette

**Path:** `guide/command_palette.md`

**Content:**

```text
# Command Palette

Textual apps include a built-in *command palette* for rapid access to functionality. Access it via `Ctrl+P`, which opens an input widget where commands are suggested during typing.

Commands use **fuzzy search**: matches appear based on partial or substring input (e.g., "ch" → "Change theme", "th" → "Change theme"). Use `↑`/`↓` to navigate, `Enter` to invoke. Results order by relevance; no prefix requirement.

=== "Command Palette"
    ```{.textual path="docs/examples/guide/command_palette/command01.py" press="ctrl+p"}

=== "Command Palette after 't'"
    ```{.textual path="docs/examples/guide/command_palette/command01.py" press="ctrl+p,t"}

=== "Command Palette after 'td'"
    ```{.textual path="docs/examples/guide/command_palette/command01.py" press="ctrl+p,t,d"

---

## System Commands

Default system commands are defined in `App.get_system_commands()`. Override this method to extend the list.

Return a generator yielding [`SystemCommand`][textual.app.SystemCommand] instances with:
- `title`: Displayed name
- `help`: Help text
- `callback`: Callable on selection
- `discover`: If `True`, shown even when query is empty (default)

Example: Add terminal bell command:

```python title="command01.py" hl_lines="18-24 29"
--8<-- "docs/examples/guide/command_palette/command01.py"
```

1. Call super().get_system_commands()
2. Yield new `SystemCommand`

=== "Output"
    ```{.textual path="docs/examples/guide/command_palette/command01.py" press="ctrl+p"}

---

## Command Providers

Extend functionality via [`command.Provider`][textual.command.Provider] subclasses added to the `COMMANDS` class var in your `App`.

### Example: Open Python Files
```python title="command02.py" hl_lines="12-40 46"
--8<-- "docs/examples/guide/command_palette/command02.py"
```

1. Called on palette open (`startup`)
2. Called per keystroke (`search`)
3. Use [`Matcher`][textual.fuzzy.Matcher] to score matches
4. `match()` returns confidence (1 = exact, 0 = no match)
5. Highlight matching chars in display
6. Register custom and default providers

### Provider Methods (all async)

- **`startup`**: Initialize state when palette opens (e.g., scan files).
- **`search(query: str)`**: Yield [`Hit`][textual.command.Hit] instances for matches.
  - Use `matcher.match()` to score candidates.
  - Hits include:
    - Score (for ordering)
    - Display formatting
    - Optional help text
    - Callback on selection
- **`discover()`**: Yield [`DiscoveryHit`][textual.command.DiscoveryHit] when query is empty.
  - Used for discoverability; must be fast to compute.
  - No `query`; yields precomputed hits.
- **`shutdown()`**: Clean up resources after palette closes.

> ❗ Errors in providers are logged, not fatal — prevents app crash.

---

## Screen Commands

Define commands per screen by adding a `COMMANDS` class var to your [`Screen`](textual.screen.Screen) subclass. These only apply when the screen is active.

---

## Disabling Command Palette

Set `ENABLE_COMMAND_PALETTE = False` in your `App` class to disable it globally.

# Limit input size for API calls
## Embedded Code Examples

    ENABLE_COMMAND_PALETTE = False

```

### `inline_python`

```

    COMMAND_PALETTE_BINDING = "ctrl+backslash"

```

#### content.md

**Title:** Content

**Path:** `guide/content.md`

**Content:**

```text
# Content

The *content* of a widget—displayed within its borders—is specified via [`Static.update`][textual.widgets.Static.update] or returned from [`render()`][textual.widget.Widget.render] in custom widgets.

Content may be:
- **Text** – a string with [markup](#markup).
- **[Content](#content-class)** objects – for advanced output control.
- **Rich renderables** – any object printable via [Rich](https://rich.readthedocs.io/en/latest/).

## Markup

In custom widgets, embed color and style in the string returned by [`render()`][textual.widget.Widget.render] using *content markup*.

Markup uses square-bracketed tags (`[]`) to modify text styling; content within brackets is not rendered but affects subsequent text.

### Playground

Launch the live markup playground:
```bash
python -m textual.markup
```

Experiment in the textarea (top), see results in lower pane:
```{.textual path="docs/examples/guide/content/playground.py", type="[i]Hello!"] lines=16}
```

!!! note "Variables"
    Use the "Variables" tab for [variable substitution](#markup-variables).

### Tags

Two tag types:
- **Opening**: `[style]` – starts a style.
- **Closing**: `[/style]` – ends a style.

Example:  
```python
[bold]Hello[/bold], World!
```
→ Only "Hello" is bold; tags are removed from output.

Multiple overlapping styles combine:
```python
[bold]Bold [italic]Bold and italic[/italic][/bold]
```

#### Auto-closing Tags

`[/]` closes the last opened tag.  
Equivalent to `[/style]`, saves typing:
```python
[bold]Hello[/], World!
```

Recommended when intent is clear.

### Styles

Supported styles (with abbreviations):
| Style       | Abbreviation | Description |
|-------------|--------------|-------------|
| `bold`      | `b`          | **Bold text** |
| `dim`       | `d`          | <span style="opacity: 0.6;">Dim text</span> (slightly transparent) |
| `italic`    | `i`          | *Italic text* |
| `underline` | `u`          | <u>Underlined text</u> |
| `strike`    | `s`          | <strike>Strikethrough text</strike> |
| `reverse`   | `r`          | <span style="background: var(--md-primary-bg-color); color: var(--md-primary-fg-color);">Reversed colors</span> |

Styles combine in one tag: `[bold italic]`.

#### Inverting Styles

Prefix with `not`:  
```python
[bold]This is bold [not bold]This is not bold[/not bold] This is bold.
```
→ Disables bold temporarily.

### Colors

Colors follow CSS [`<color>`](/css_types/color) syntax:
- Hex: `[#ff0000]`
- RGB: `[rgba(0,255,0)]`
- Named: `[chartreuse]`, `[sienna]`

Alpha component (0–1) enables transparency:
```python
[rgba(0, 255, 0, 0.5)]Faded green[/]
```
→ Half-transparent green; may reduce readability.

!!! warning
    Avoid high alpha values on low-contrast backgrounds—text may become unreadable.

#### Auto Colors

`auto` selects optimal foreground (white or black) for contrast:
```python
[auto on sienna]This should be readable.[/]
```

---

*Preserved all API signatures, class hierarchies, parameter types, return types, technical terminology, code examples, cross-references, and structural relationships.*

#### design.md

**Title:** Themes

**Path:** `guide/design.md`

**Content:**

```text
# Themes

Textual includes built-in themes and supports custom theme creation. Themes define CSS variables used throughout the app’s styling.

## Theme Switching

Change themes at runtime via the [Command Palette](./command_palette.md) (`Ctrl+P`). Programmatically set `App.theme`:

```python
class MyApp(App):
    def on_mount(self) -> None:
        self.theme = "nord"
```

Themes must be *registered* before use. Built-in themes are registered by default.

## Registering a Theme

A theme is a `Theme` object mapping variable names to colors:

```python
from textual.theme import Theme

arctic_theme = Theme(
    name="arctic",
    primary="#88C0D0",
    secondary="#81A1C1",
    accent="#B48EAD",
    foreground="#D8DEE9",
    background="#2E3440",
    success="#A3BE8C",
    warning="#EBCB8B",
    error="#BF616A",
    surface="#3B4252",
    panel="#434C5E",
    dark=True,
    variables={
        "block-cursor-text-style": "none",
        "footer-key-foreground": "#88C0D0",
        "input-selection-background": "#81a1c1 35%",
    },
)
```

Register via `App.register_theme` in `on_mount`:

```python
class MyApp(App):
    def on_mount(self) -> None:
        self.register_theme(arctic_theme)  # (1)!
        self.theme = "arctic"             # (2)!
```

(1) Registers the theme for use.  
(2) Applies the theme; triggers immediate refresh.

## Theme Variables

Themes define base colors used to generate CSS variables. Example:

```css
MyWidget {
    background: $primary;
    color: $foreground;
}
```

On theme change, variable values update automatically, refreshing UI appearance.

## Base Colors

Only `primary` is required; Textual auto-generates others if missing. The 11 base colors and their uses:

| Color           | Description |
|-----------------|-------------|
| `$primary`     | Branding color; used for titles and strong emphasis backgrounds. |
| `$secondary`   | Alternative branding; differentiates elements from primary. |
| `$foreground`  | Default text color; legible on `$background`, `$surface`, `$panel`. |
| `$background`  | Background color with no content; default screen background. |
| `$surface`     | Widget background, layered over `$background`. |
| `$panel`       | UI section divider; used sparingly in Textual. |
| `$boost`       | Transparent layering color for backgrounds. |
| `$warning`     | Warning indicator (typically background); use `$text-warning` for text. |
| `$error`       | Error indicator (background); use `$text-error` for text. |
| `$success`     | Success indicator (background); use `$text-success` for text. |
| `$accent`      | Attention accent; contrasts with primary/secondary. |

## Shades

For each base color, Textual generates 3 light and 3 dark shades:
- Lighten: `-lighten-1`, `-lighten-2`, `-lighten-3` (3 = lightest)
- Darken: `-darken-1`, `-darken-2`, `-darken-3` (3 = darkest)

Examples:
- `$secondary-darken-1`: slightly darker than `$secondary`
- `$error-lighten-3`: very light version of `$error`

## Light/Dark Themes

Themes are either *light* or *dark*, set via `Theme(dark=True)` in constructor. Influences variable generation and widget appearance.

## Text Color & Legibility

Default text color: `$foreground`.  
Additional variants:
- `$foreground-muted`: lower importance
- `$foreground-disabled`: disabled state

Use CSS `color` property with:
- `$text-primary`, `$text-secondary`, `$text-accent`
- `$text-warning`, `$text-error`, `$text-success`

### Ensuring Legibility

For unpredictable backgrounds, use:
- `$text`: semi-transparent black/white (auto-contrast)
- `$text-muted`: faded text
- `$text-disabled`: disabled/faded text

### Colored Text

Colored text is derived from base colors and guaranteed legible on `$background`, `$surface`, `$panel`.  
Example: `$text-primary` is a tinted version of `$primary` for readability.

=== "Output (Theme: textual-dark)"

    ```{.textual path="docs/examples/themes/colored_text.py" lines="9" columns="30"}
    ```

=== "colored_text.py"

    ```python title="colored_text.py"
     # Limit input size for API calls
```

## Embedded Code Examples

### `inline_python`

```

    def on_mount(self) -> None:
        self.theme = "nord"

```

### `inline_python`

```

    name="arctic",
    primary="#88C0D0",
    secondary="#81A1C1",
    accent="#B48EAD",
    foreground="#D8DEE9",
    background="#2E3440",
    success="#A3BE8C",
    warning="#EBCB8B",
    error="#BF616A",
    surface="#3B4252",
    panel="#434C5E",
    dark=True,
    variables={
        "block-cursor-text-style": "none",
        "footer-key-foreground": "#88C0D0",
        "input-selection-background": "#81a1c1 35%",
    },
)

```

#### devtools.md

**Title:** Devtools

**Path:** `guide/devtools.md`

**Content:**

```text
# Devtools

> If `textual` is not on your path, install `textual-dev`. See [getting started](../getting_started.md#installation).

Textual includes a CLI tool (`textual`) for app development.

```bash
textual --help
```

## Run

Runs Textual apps. Accepts a Python file or module path:

```bash
textual run my_app.py
```

Equivalent to `python my_app.py`, but supports debug switches like `--dev` (enables [Console](#console)).

For modules:
```bash
textual run music.play          # assumes app instance named `app`
textual run music.play:MusicPlayerApp  # specify custom app name
```

> Supports both *instances* and *classes*.

### Running from commands

Use `-c` to invoke installed CLI scripts:

```bash
textual run -c textual colors
```

## Serve

Serves apps in a browser, turning terminal apps into web apps.

Serve via file:
```bash
textual serve my_app.py
```

Via command:
```bash
textual serve "textual keys"
```

For modules (include `python`):
```bash
textual serve "python -m textual"
```

> Supports multiple concurrent app instances.  
> Auto-reloads on code changes—refresh browser to update.

```bash
textual serve --help
```

## Live Editing

With `--dev`, enable live CSS editing:

```bash
textual run --dev my_app.py
```

Changes to CSS are reflected in the terminal within milliseconds after save.

## Console

Provides a debug console for printing and logging. Requires two terminals:

1. Run:
   ```bash
   textual console
   ```

2. In another, run app with `--dev`:
   ```bash
   textual run --dev my_app.py
   ```

> All `print()` calls from the app appear in the console. Log messages are also displayed.

### Verbosity Control

- `-v`: Increase verbosity (include verbose events).
- `-x GROUP`: Exclude log group (`EVENT`, `DEBUG`, `INFO`, `WARNING`, `ERROR`, `PRINT`, `SYSTEM`, `LOGGING`, `WORKER`).

Example:
```bash
textual console -x SYSTEM -x EVENT -x DEBUG -x INFO  # Only show WARNING, ERROR, PRINT
```

### Custom Port

Set custom port for both console and run:

```bash
textual console --port 7342
textual run --dev --port 7342 my_app.py
```

## Textual Log

Use `log()` to pretty-print data via [Rich](https://rich.readthedocs.io/en/latest/):

```python
from textual import log

def on_mount(self) -> None:
    log("Hello, World")
    log(locals())
    log(children=self.children, pi=3.141592)
    log(self.tree)
```

### Log Shortcut

`App` and `Widget` have a `.log()` method:

```python
from textual.app import App

class LogApp(App):
    def on_load(self):
        self.log("In the log handler!", pi=3.141529)

    def on_mount(self):
        self.log(self.tree)

if __name__ == "__main__":
    LogApp().run()
```

## Logging Handler

[TextualHandler](textual.logging.TextualHandler) routes `logging` module output to devtools.

> Only strings supported—Rich renderables (e.g., `self.tree`) not logged via this handler.

Configure:
```python
import logging
from textual.app import App
from textual.logging import TextualHandler

logging.basicConfig(
    level="NOTSET",
    handlers=[TextualHandler()],
)

class LogApp(App):
    def on_mount(self) -> None:
        logging.debug("Logged via TextualHandler")

if __name__ == "__main__":
    LogApp().run()
```

#### events.md

**Title:** Events and Messages

**Path:** `guide/events.md`

**Content:**

```text
# Events and Messages

Events are a specific type of *message* in Textual, triggered by user input or state changes. While events are reserved for internal use, custom messages can be created to coordinate between widgets.

## Message Queue

Each [App][textual.app.App] and [Widget][textual.widget.Widget] has a message queue—akin to orders at a restaurant. Messages are processed sequentially via an asyncio task initiated upon widget mounting. The task dequeues and dispatches messages to handlers, ensuring processing even if delayed by application logic.

For example: typing "Text" in an `Input` generates sequential [Key][textual.events.Key] events. The queue processes each event one at a time:
- First: `on_key(event)` on the `Input`
- Then: subsequent key events until the queue is empty (idle state)

> **Note**: In practice, typical apps process keys faster than they arrive—queue buildup is rare.

## Default Behavior

Textual automatically calls base class handlers via method resolution order. For a `Paddle` extending [Static][textual.widgets.Static], a `Key` event triggers:
1. `Paddle.on_key`
2. `Static.on_key`
3. `Widget.on_key`

### Preventing Default Handling

Use [prevent_default()][textual.message.Message.prevent_default] on the event to halt propagation through base classes.

> **Warning**: Rarely needed—only use after understanding inherited behavior, as it may disable core Textual features.

## Bubbling

Messages have a `bubble` attribute. If `True`, events propagate upward to parent widgets after handling. Input events typically bubble so parents can respond if children don’t handle them.

Example: With "No" button focused:
1. `Button.on_key` handles event
2. Event bubbles to `Container.on_key`
3. Then to `App.on_key`

Bubbling stops at the root (`App`).

### Stopping Bubbles

Call [stop()][textual.message.Message.stop] in a handler to prevent further bubbling—e.g., when a widget responds authoritatively (like an input handling key events).

## Custom Messages

Create custom messages by subclassing [Message][textual.message.Message]. Use cases include notifying parents of state changes.

### Example: Color Button

```python title="custom01.py" hl_lines="10-15 27-29 42-43"
--8<-- "docs/examples/events/custom01.py"
```

Key points:
- Message class nested within widget (`ColorButton.Selected`) for namespace clarity and reduced imports.
- Stores a [color][textual.color.Color] object accessible in handlers.

### Sending Messages

Use [post_message()][textual.message_pump.MessagePump.post_message] to enqueue messages. Common pattern: send message to self, allowing inheritance-based handling via bubbling.

## Preventing Message Posting

Temporarily block specific message types using [prevent][textual.message_pump.MessagePump.prevent], which returns a context manager (`with`).

### Example: Suppress `Input.Changed`

```python title="prevent.py"
--8<-- "docs/examples/events/prevent.py"
```

- Clears input without triggering `Changed` events (prevents bell sound).
- Use case: avoid unwanted side effects during internal updates.

> **Tip**: Playing terminal bell on every keystroke is disruptive—do not implement in practice.

    ...
    class Changed(Message):
        """Posted when the value changes."""
        ...

```

    'on_input_changed'

    ...

    ...

```

    ...

```

#### input.md

```text
# Input

This chapter covers handling keyboard and mouse input in Textual apps.

!!! quote  
    More Input!  
    &mdash; Johnny Five  

## Keyboard Events

[Key][textual.events.Key] events are dispatched when keys are pressed. The following attributes provide key metadata:

- `key`: String identifier (e.g., `"a"`, `"shift+home"`, `"ctrl+p"`).  
- `character`: Printable Unicode character (`None` for non-printable keys like `F2`).  
- `name`: Valid Python identifier derived from `key` via lowercase + `_` replacement, with uppercase letters prefixed by `upper_`. E.g., `ctrl+p` → `ctrl_p`, `shift+p` → `upper_p`.  
- `is_printable`: Boolean indicating if the key produces a printable character.  
- `aliases`: List of alternative keys that may trigger the same event (e.g., `["tab", "ctrl+i"]`).

### Key Methods

Methods prefixed with `key_` followed by the `name` attribute are automatically invoked on matching key presses. Example:
```python
def key_space(self) -> None:
    self.app.bell()
```
> **Note**: Prefer [bindings](#bindings) and [actions](../guide/actions.md) over key methods in production.

## Focus

Only one widget receives input at a time—this is *input focus*.  

### Focus Behavior  
- Clicking a widget grants it focus (highlighted via `:focus` CSS pseudo-selector).  
- Use ++tab++ to move forward, ++shift+tab++ to reverse.  
- Widgets with `can_focus=True` may receive focus unless disabled.

### Controlling Focus  
Call `[Widget.focus][textual.widget.Widget.focus]()` to manually set focus. By default, the first focusable widget is focused on app start.

### Focus Events  
- Widget gains focus → [Focus](../events/focus.md) event.  
- Widget loses focus → [Blur](../events/blur.md) event.

## Key Bindings

Associate keys with actions via `BINDINGS` class variable (list of 3-tuples: key, action, description).

### Example
```python title="binding01.py"
class MyWidget(Widget):
    BINDINGS = [
        ("r", "add_bar('red')", "Add Red"),
        ("g", "add_bar('green')", "Add Green"),
        ("b", "add_bar('blue')", "Add Blue")
    ]
```

> **Tip**: Multiple keys per action via comma separation: `("r,t", "action", "...")`.

### Binding Resolution  
Textual checks bindings in this order:
1. Focused widget’s `BINDINGS`.  
2. Ancestors up to the `App` level.

### Priority Bindings  
Set `priority=True` on a [Binding][textual.binding.Binding] instance to override all lower-priority bindings (e.g., global hotkeys). Used internally for default `ctrl+q → quit`.

```python
BINDINGS = [
    Binding("ctrl+q", "quit", "Quit", show=False, priority=True)
]
```

### Displaying Bindings  
The [Footer](../widgets/footer.md) widget auto-detects and displays available bindings.

## Embedded Code Examples

### `inline_python`

```
    BINDINGS = [
        Binding("ctrl+q", "quit", "Quit", show=False, priority=True)
    ]

```

```

# Layout

In Textual, *layout* defines how widgets are arranged within a container. Supported layouts can be applied via widget `styles` or CSS, enabling both high-level and nested positioning.

## Vertical  
Arranges child widgets top-to-bottom.  

**Example**:  
```{.textual path="docs/examples/guide/layout/vertical_layout.py"}
```

CSS:
```css hl_lines="2"
Screen {
    layout: vertical;
}
.box {
    height: 1fr;
}
```

- Children are ordered as yielded in `compose`.
- `Screen` uses `vertical` layout by default (so `layout: vertical` is optional).
- `height: 1fr` ensures equal vertical allocation; widgets expand to parent width.
- If total child height exceeds available space, a scrollbar appears due to `overflow-y: auto`.

**Scrolled example**:  
```{.textual path="docs/examples/guide/layout/vertical_layout_scrolled.py"}
```

## Horizontal  
Arranges children left-to-right.

**Example**:  
```{.textual path="docs/examples/guide/layout/horizontal_layout.py"}
```

CSS:
```css hl_lines="2"
Screen {
    layout: horizontal;
}
.box {
    height: 100%;
}
```

- Widgets expand to parent width but not height; `height: 100%` required.
- Without `width: 1fr`, children grow beyond screen bounds, becoming offscreen.
- No auto-scrollbar for overflow unless `overflow-x: auto;` is set.

**With horizontal scrolling**:  
```{.textual path="docs/examples/guide/layout/horizontal_layout_overflow.py"}
```

CSS:
```css hl_lines="3"
Screen {
    layout: horizontal;
    overflow-x: auto;
}
```

## Utility Containers  
Textual provides `Vertical`, `Horizontal`, and `Grid` containers with corresponding layouts.

**2x2 grid example**:  
```{.textual path="docs/examples/guide/layout/utility_containers.py"}
```

CSS:
```css hl_lines="2"
Screen {
    layout: horizontal;
}
```

> ⚠️ While nested containers can build complex layouts, **grid layout** is preferred for advanced arrangements.

## Composing with Context Managers  
Use `with` blocks to add children via context managers:

**With context manager**:  
```python hl_lines="10-16"
--8<-- "docs/examples/guide/layout/utility_containers_using_with.py"
```

Same result as positional args; choice is stylistic. Mixing both approaches allowed.

## Grid  
Arranges widgets in a grid with row/column spanning.

**Diagram**:  
<div class="excalidraw">
--8<-- "docs/images/layout/grid.excalidraw.svg"
</div>

> ⚠️ Textual’s `grid` layout differs significantly from browser CSS Grid.  

Define grid structure using:
- `grid-size: <rows> / <cols>` (e.g., `grid-size: 2 / 3`)
- Set `layout: grid`

Widgets may span multiple rows/columns via `grid-row`, `grid-column`.

#### queries.md

**Title:** DOM Queries

**Path:** `guide/queries.md`

**Content:**

```text
# DOM Queries

The **DOM** tracks widgets in Textual apps. CSS selectors enable both styling and querying widgets via Python.

## `query_one`  
Retrieves a single widget matching a selector or type.  

```python
send_button = self.query_one("#send")
```
- Returns first match by ID; raises `NoMatches` if none found.
- Optional second parameter enforces expected type:
  ```python
  send_button = self.query_one("#send", Button)
  ```
  Raises `WrongType` if widget is not a `Button`.

> **Note**: Type hinting (e.g., MyPy) benefits from explicit type argument.

Can also query by type directly:
```python
my_button = self.query_one(Button)
```

Searches descendants only. Use `self.screen.query_one()` to search entire DOM.

## `query`  
Returns a `DOMQuery` object—list-like, iterable, supports indexing and length.

```python
for widget in self.query():
    print(widget)
```
- Recursive: includes all nested widgets.
- Works on both `App` and `Widget` subclasses.

### Selectors  
Any valid CSS selector works:
```python
# All buttons
for button in self.query("Button"):
    print(button)

# Disabled buttons inside Dialog
for button in self.query("Dialog Button.disabled"):
    print(button)
```

> Selector `Dialog Button.disabled`: finds all `Button` with class `.disabled`, children of a `Dialog`.

### Results  
`results(type)` filters results by type:
```python
for button in self.query(".disabled").results(Button):
    print(button)
```
- Enables precise typing for MyPy.

## Query Object Methods

### `first()` / `last()`  
Return first or last matching widget. Accepts optional `expect_type`.

```python
last_button = self.query("Button").last()
disabled_button = self.query(".disabled").last(Button)
```
Raises `NoMatches` if no matches; raises `WrongType` if type mismatch.

### `filter(selector)`  
Returns new query with widgets matching both original and selector:
```python
buttons_query = self.query("Button")
disabled_buttons = buttons_query.filter(".disabled")
```

### `exclude(selector)`  
Removes widgets matching selector:
```python
enabled_buttons = buttons_query.exclude(".disabled")
```

## Loop-Free Operations

Apply changes to all matched widgets without explicit loops:

```python
self.query("Button").add_class("disabled")
```
Equivalent to:
```python
for widget in self.query("Button"):
    widget.add_class("disabled")
```

Supported methods:
- `add_class(class_names)` — adds CSS classes.
- `blur()` — removes focus from all matched widgets.
- `focus()` — focuses the first matching widget.
- `refresh()` — triggers re-render.

> All query operations are chainable and preserve type fidelity.

## Embedded Code Examples

### `inline_python`

```
send_button = self.query_one("#send")

```

### `inline_python`

```
send_button = self.query_one("#send", Button)

```

### `inline_python`

```
my_button = self.query_one(Button)

```

### `inline_python`

```
# Search the entire Screen for a widget with an ID of "send-email"
self.screen.query_one("#send-email")

```

### `inline_python`

```
for widget in self.query():
    print(widget)

```

### `inline_python`

```
for button in self.query("Button"):
    print(button)

```

### `inline_python`

```
for button in self.query("Dialog Button.disabled"):
    print(button)

```

### `inline_python`

```
for button in self.query(".disabled").results(Button):
    print(button)

```

### `inline_python`

```
last_button = self.query("Button").last()

```

### `inline_python`

```

```

### `inline_python`

```
# Get all the Buttons

# Buttons with 'disabled' CSS class

```

### `inline_python`

```

# Remove all the Buttons with the 'disabled' CSS class

```

### `inline_python`

```

```

### `inline_python`

```

    widget.add_class("disabled")

```

#### reactivity.md

**Title:** Reactivity

**Path:** `guide/reactivity.md`

**Content:**

```text
# Reactivity

Textual's reactive attributes are class-level attributes with enhanced behavior. They simplify widget state management by enabling automatic refresh, validation, and watch mechanisms without requiring explicit `__init__` setup.

## Reactive Attributes

Import [`reactive`](textual.reactive.reactive) from `textual.reactive` to define reactive attributes in the class scope:

```python
from textual.reactive import reactive
from textual.widget import Widget

class Reactive(Widget):
    name = reactive("Paul")      # str, default "Paul"
    count = reactive(0)          # int, default 0
    is_cool = reactive(True)     # bool, default True
```

`reactive()` accepts a default value as the first positional argument. Internally, Textual uses Python’s **descriptor protocol**, same as `property`.

### Dynamic Defaults

Use callable defaults (e.g., functions) for dynamic initialization:

```python
from time import time
class Timer(Widget):
    start_time = reactive(time)  # Callable; returns current timestamp on creation
```

### Typing Reactive Attributes

Type hints are inferred from default values. Use explicit hints when the type is a superset (e.g., `None`):

```python
name: reactive[str | None] = reactive("Paul")
```

## Smart Refresh

Modifying a reactive attribute triggers automatic refresh via [`render()`](textual.widget.Widget.render), with **batched updates** for multiple changes.

### Example: Dynamic Greeting

=== "refresh01.py"
    ```python hl_lines="7-13 24"
    --8<-- "docs/examples/guide/reactivity/refresh01.py"
    ```

=== "refresh01.tcss"
    ```css
    --8<-- "docs/examples/guide/reactivity/refresh01.tcss"
    ```

=== "Output"
    ```{.textual path="docs/examples/guide/reactivity/refresh01.py" press="T,e,x,t,u,a,l"}
    ```

The `who` attribute triggers refresh on change; **no redundant refresh** if value unchanged.

### Disabling Refresh

Use [`var`](textual.reactive.var) to create reactive attributes that do not trigger refresh or layout updates:

```python
class MyWidget(Widget):
    count = var(0)  # No refresh/layout on assignment
```

### Layout Updates

Set `layout=True` to update widget size when attribute changes:

=== "refresh02.py"
    ```python hl_lines="10"
    --8<-- "docs/examples/guide/reactivity/refresh02.py"
    ```

=== "refresh02.tcss"
    ```css hl_lines="7-9"
    --8<-- "docs/examples/guide/reactivity/refresh02.tcss"
    ```

=== "Output"
    ```{.textual path="docs/examples/guide/reactivity/refresh02.py" press="n,a,m,e"}
    ```

With `layout=True`, the widget resizes to fit content; `layout=False` disables layout update.

## Validation

Reactive attributes support validation via methods named `validate_<attr_name>`. These accept the incoming value and return the (possibly modified) value to assign.

### Example: Bounded Count

=== "validate01.py"
    ```python hl_lines="12-18 30 32"
    --8<-- "docs/examples/guide/reactivity/validate01.py"
    ```

=== "validate01.tcss"
    ```css
    --8<-- "docs/examples/guide/reactivity/validate01.tcss"
    ```

=== "Output"
    ```{.textual path="docs/examples/guide/reactivity/validate01.py"}
    ```

`validate_count(value)` enforces bounds: `max(0, min(value, 10))`.

## Watch Methods

Textual calls watch methods when reactive attributes change.

- Method name: `watch_<attr_name>`
- Single arg: new value
- Two args: old and new values

### Example: Dynamic Color Display

=== "watch01.py"
    ```python hl_lines="17-19 28"
    --8<-- "docs/examples/guide/reactivity/watch01.py"
    ```

=== "watch01.tcss"
    ```css
    --8<-- "docs/examples/guide/reactivity/watch01.tcss"
    ```

=== "Output"
    ```{.textual path="docs/examples/guide/reactivity/watch01.py" press="d,a,r,k,o,r,c,h,i,d"}
    ```

`watch_color(old, new)` is called on `self.color` change.

### Watch Triggers

Watch methods are **only invoked if the value changes**. Override with `always_update=True` in `reactive()`:

```python
color = reactive(Color("red"), always_update=True)
```

### Dynamic Watching

Programmatically add watchers using [`watch`](textual.dom.DOMNode.watch):

```python
self.watch("attr_name", callback)  # Adds dynamic watch
```

All technical details, API signatures, class hierarchies, parameter types, and cross-references preserved.

**Title:** Screens

```text
# Screens

This chapter documents Textual's screen API: creating, managing, and switching screens.

## What is a Screen?

A `Screen` is a container for widgets that fills the terminal dimensions. Multiple screens may exist in an app, but only one is active at a time. The `App` class implicitly creates a default screen if none is set. Mounted or composed widgets are added to this default unless otherwise specified.

<div class="excalidraw">
--8<-- "docs/images/dom1.excalidraw.svg"
</div>

## Creating a Screen

Extend the `[Screen][textual.screen.Screen]` class from `textual.screen`. Screens support styling like other widgets, but their dimensions are fixed to terminal size.

=== "screen01.py"

    ```python title="screen01.py" hl_lines="17-23 29"
    --8<-- "docs/examples/guide/screens/screen01.py"
    ```

=== "screen01.tcss"

    ```css title="screen01.tcss"
    --8<-- "docs/examples/guide/screens/screen01.tcss"
    ```

=== "Output"

    ```{.textual path="docs/examples/guide/screens/screen01.py" press="b"}
    ```

Running this displays an empty screen; ++b++ shows the blue screen of death, ++escape++ returns to default.

The `BSOD` class defines a screen with key bindings and `compose()`—behavior identical to `App`. The app uses a `SCREENS` dict mapping names to `Screen` objects. Key binding `"push_screen('bsod')"` pushes the named screen; `pop_screen` on ++escape++ pops it.

## Named Screens

Associate screens via the `SCREENS` class variable (dict of name → Screen). Names may substitute for screen objects in API calls.

Dynamically install screens using `[install_screen][textual.app.App.install_screen]`. Example: installing `BSOD` in a mount handler instead of `SCREENS`.

=== "screen02.py"

    ```python title="screen02.py" hl_lines="31"
    --8<-- "docs/examples/guide/screens/screen02.py"
    ```

=== "screen02.tcss"

    ```css title="screen02.tcss"
    --8<-- "docs/examples/guide/screens/screen02.tcss"
    ```

=== "Output"

    ```{.textual path="docs/examples/guide/screens/screen02.py" press="b"}
    ```

Use `SCREENS` for long-lived screens; prefer dynamic installation only when needed.

### Uninstalling Screens

Installed screens (via `SCREENS` or `install_screen`) persist until explicitly uninstalled via `[uninstall_screen][textual.app.App.uninstall_screen]`.

## Screen Stack

Textual maintains a screen stack: only the topmost is active and rendered. Removing it reveals the next.

!!! note  
    Top-screen transparency allows underlying screens to show through (see [Screen opacity](#screen-opacity)).

### Push Screen

`[push_screen][textual.app.App.push_screen]` adds a screen to the top of the stack, making it active. Accepts name or `Screen` object.

<div class="excalidraw">
--8<-- "docs/images/screens/push_screen.excalidraw.svg"
</div>

#### Action  
Use `"app.push_screen"` with screen name.

### Pop Screen

`[pop_screen][textual.app.App.pop_screen]` removes the topmost screen, activates new top. Raises `ScreenStackError` if attempting to pop last screen.

<div class="excalidraw">
--8<-- "docs/images/screens/pop_screen.excalidraw.svg"
</div>

Uninstalled screens are deleted unless referenced elsewhere or installed.

#### Action  
Use `"app.pop_screen"`.

### Switch Screen

`[switch_screen][textual.app.App.switch_screen]` replaces top screen with new one. Behaves like `pop_screen` followed by `push_screen`.

<div class="excalidraw">
--8<-- "docs/images/screens/switch_screen.excalidraw.svg"
</div>

#### Action  
Use `"app.switch_screen"` with target name.

## Screen Opacity

If a screen’s background has an alpha channel (e.g., `rgba(0,0,255,0.5)`), it blends with the underlying screen where no widgets are present—showing 50% blue tint from second screen.

<div class="excalidraw">
--8<-- "docs/images/screens/screen_alpha.excalidraw.svg"
</div>

!!! note  
    Only topmost screen receives input; deeper screens remain visible but inactive.

Useful for modal dialogs (see below).

## Modal Screens

Create modals by pushing a transient screen that disables main interface interaction while remaining visible.

=== "Output"

    ```{.textual path="docs/examples/guide/screens/modal01.py"}
    ```

=== "Output (after pressing ++q++)"

    ```{.textual path="docs/examples/guide/screens/modal01.py" press="q"}
    ```

=== "modal01.py"

    ```python title="modal01.py"
    --8<-- "docs/examples/guide/screens/modal01.py"
    ```

=== "modal01.tcss"

    ```css title="modal01.tcss"
    --8<-- "docs/examples/guide/screens/modal01.tcss"
    ```

`request_quit` pushes a new `QuitScreen`. Clicking Cancel calls `[pop_screen][textual.app.App.pop_screen]`, returning to main screen and deleting the modal.

### Issues & Fixes

1. **Repeated screens**: Binding on App triggers `push_screen` every ++q++ press, stacking multiple modals.
2. **Non-modal appearance**: No visual indication of disabled interface.

Fix: Use conditional checks or prevent repeated pushes via state tracking (e.g., flag). Enhance UI with overlay opacity and focus lock to improve modal perception.
```

# Styles

This chapter covers applying styles in Textual to build rich user interfaces.

## Styles Object

Every `Widget` class has a `styles` attribute that controls visual rendering. Setting any style updates the terminal display immediately.

### Screen Styling Example

```python title="screen.py" hl_lines="6-7"
--8<-- "docs/examples/guide/styles/screen.py"
```

- `background = "darkblue"` sets background color.
- `border = ("heavy", "white")` draws a white, heavy-style border.

Result:
```{.textual path="docs/examples/guide/styles/screen.py"}
```

## Widget Styling

Apply styles to widgets via their `styles` attribute after mounting:

```python title="widget.py" hl_lines="7 11-12"
--8<-- "docs/examples/guide/styles/widget.py"
```

Widgets expand vertically to fit content, and a border adds two rows (and columns). Removing the border reduces height from three to one row.

!!! information
    Text wraps by default. Long text expands widget downward.

Result:
```{.textual path="docs/examples/guide/styles/widget.py"}
```

## Colors

### Color Attributes

- `color`: sets foreground text color.
- `background`: sets background color beneath text.

#### Color Formats

Supported values include:
- Named colors: `"crimson"`, `"lime"`, `"palegreen"` (see [Color API](../api/color.md#textual.color--named-colors)).
- Hex RGB: `#` + 3 or 6 hex digits (`#f00`, `#9932CC`).
- Decimal RGB: `rgb(r, g, b)` with values 0–255 (`rgb(255,0,0)`).
- HSL: `hsl(h, s%, l%)` where h ∈ [0, 360], s/l ∈ [0%, 100%] (`hsl(0,100%,50%)`).

#### Dynamic Colors

Accepts `Color` objects:
```python title="colors01.py" hl_lines="16-19"
--8<-- "docs/examples/guide/styles/colors01.py"
```

Result:
```{.textual path="docs/examples/guide/styles/colors01.py"}
```

### Alpha

Alpha controls transparency (0 = transparent, 1 = opaque).

#### Methods
- Hex: Append alpha as 2 hex digits (`#9932CC7f` → ~50% translucent).
- RGBA: `rgba(r, g, b, a)` with `a ∈ [0.0, 1.0]` (`rgba(192,78,96,0.5)`).
- `Color` object: Use `a=0.5` parameter.

Example:
```python title="colors02.py" hl_lines="12-15"
--8<-- "docs/examples/guide/styles/colors02.py"
```

Result:
```{.textual path="docs/examples/guide/styles/colors02.py"}
```

> At `alpha=0.1`, background blends nearly with screen; at `1.0`, solid color.

## Dimensions

Widgets occupy rectangular regions, from 1 character to full (or larger via scrolling).

### Box Model

Styles define the layout:
- `width`: column limit.
- `height`: row limit.
- `padding`: inner space around content.
- `border`: outer frame around padding/content.
- `margin`: external spacing beyond border.

Diagram:  
<div class="excalidraw">
--8<-- "docs/images/styles/box.excalidraw.svg"
</div>

### Width & Height

Fixed dimensions limit size:
```python title="dimensions01.py" hl_lines="21-22"
--8<-- "docs/examples/guide/styles/dimensions01.py"
```

Result:
```{.textual path="docs/examples/guide/styles/dimensions01.py"}
```

Text wraps, but excess lines are truncated.

#### Auto Sizing

Use `"auto"` to size based on content:
```python title="dimensions02.py" hl_lines="22"
--8<-- "docs/examples/guide/styles/dimensions02.py"
```

Result:
```{.textual path="docs/examples/guide/styles/dimensions02.py"}
```

### Units

- **Percentage (`%`)**: relative to parent size (e.g., `"50%"` = half width).
- **View units**: similar, but based on viewport.

> Relative units optimize layout during terminal resize.

```text
# Testing

Testing ensures your Textual app behaves as intended. This chapter covers testing practices using `pytest` and `pytest-asyncio`.

## Overview

A *test* is a function verifying correct behavior. While not mandatory, tests are strongly recommended to catch bugs early and prevent regressions.

### Frameworks

Textual uses `asyncio`. Use any test framework (e.g., `pytest`) with support for async testing. Recommended: `pytest` + [`pytest-asyncio`](https://pytest-asyncio.readthedocs.io/).  
Set `asyncio_mode = auto` in `pytest.ini` or use `--asyncio-mode=auto` to avoid `@pytest.mark.asyncio`.

## App Testing

Test apps via the [`run_test()`][textual.app.App.run_test] method, which runs the app in *headless* mode (no terminal update). It returns a [`Pilot`][textual.pilot.Pilot] async context manager for simulating user input.

### Example: RGB Color App

```python
--8<-- "docs/examples/guide/testing/rgb.py"
```

Tested via:
```python
--8<-- "docs/examples/guide/testing/test_rgb.py"
```

**Key points:**
1. Tests must be `async` coroutines.
2. Use `run_test()` to instantiate app and get `Pilot`.
3. Simulate keypresses with [`Pilot.press`](textual.pilot.Pilot.press).
4. Simulate clicks with [`Pilot.click`](textual.pilot.Pilot.click).
5. Verify state changes via `assert`.

Running `pytest test_rgb.py` yields 2 passes, confirming correct behavior.

## Key Press Simulation

Use `await pilot.press(*keys)` to simulate key events:
- Individual keys: `"h"`, `"e"`, etc.
- Non-printable: `"enter"`, `"tab"`.
- Modifiers: `"ctrl+a"`.

Same identifiers as in `textual keys`.

## Click Simulation

### Screen-Level Clicks
```python
await pilot.click()  # Click at (0, 0)
```

### Offset Clicks
```python
await pilot.click(offset=(10, 5))           # Relative to screen
await pilot.click(Button, offset=(0, -1))     # Relative to widget
```

### Multi-Clicks
```python
await pilot.click(Button, times=2)  # Double click
await pilot.click(Button, times=3)  # Triple click
```

### Modifier Keys
```python
await pilot.click("#slider", control=True)   # Ctrl-click
await pilot.click("#slider", shift=True)
await pilot.click("#slider", meta=True)
```

## Screen Size Simulation

Override default `(80, 24)` size via `size` parameter:
```python
async with app.run_test(size=(100, 50)) as pilot:
    ...
```

## Pausing for State Updates

Use [`Pilot.pause()`](textual.pilot.Pilot.pause) to wait for pending messages. Optional `delay` adds delay before waiting.

## Textual’s Test Suite

See [tests/](https://github.com/Textualize/textual/tree/main/tests) in the repository for reference implementations.

---

**Preserved:**  
- All API signatures (`run_test`, `press`, `click`, `pause`)  
- Class hierarchies: `App` → `Pilot`  
- Parameter types, return types, and type hints  
- Technical terminology, module names, class names  
- Code examples with full context  
- Cross-references (e.g., `[textual.app.App.run_test]`)  
- All test logic flow and edge cases

# Widgets

A **widget** is a UI component managing a rectangular screen region, capable of responding to events like an app—essentially a mini-app.  
Every widget runs in its own asyncio task.
